    "title": "How to backup and restore MySQL databases using the mysqldump command",
    "description": "Generate backup using mysqldump utility\r\nmysqldump -u [user name] ‚Äìp [password] [options] [database_name] [tablename] > [dumpfilename.sql]\r\n\r\nThe parameters are as following:\r\n\r\n    -u [user_name]: It is a username to connect to the MySQL server. To generate the backup using mysqldump, ‚ÄòSelect‚Äò to dump the tables, ‚ÄòShow View‚Äò for views, ‚ÄòTrigger‚Äò for the triggers. If you are not using ‚Äîsingle-transaction option, then ‚ÄòLock Tables‚Äò privileges must be granted to the user\r\n    -p [password]: The valid password of the MySQL user\r\n    [option]: The configuration option to customize the backup\r\n    [database name]: Name of the database that you want to take backup\r\n    [table name]: This is an optional parameter. If you want to take the backup specific tables, then you can specify the names in the command\r\n    ‚Äú<‚Äù OR ‚Äù>‚Äù: This character indicates whether we are generating the backup of the database or restoring the database. You can use ‚Äú>‚Äù to generate the backup and ‚Äú<‚Äù to restore the backup\r\n    [dumpfilename.sql]: Path and name of the backup file. As I mentioned, we can generate the backup in XML, delimited text, or SQL file so we can provide the extension of the file accordingly \r\n\r\nGenerate the backup of a single database\r\nmysqldump -u root -p sakila > C:\\MySQLBackup\\sakila_20200424.sql\r\n\r\nGenerate the backup of multiple databases or all the databases\r\n\r\nmysqldump -u root -p ‚Äìdatabases sakila employees > C:\\MySQLBackup\\sakila_employees_20200424.sql\r\nor for all databases\r\nmysqldump -u root -p ‚Äìall-databases > C:\\MySQLBackup\\all_databases_20200424.sql\r\n\r\nGenerate the backup of database structure\r\nysqldump -u root -p ‚Äìno-data sakila > C:\\MySQLBackup\\sakila_objects_definition_20200424.sql\r\n\r\n\r\nGenerate the backup of a specific table\r\nmysqldump -u root -p sakila actor payment > C:\\MySQLBackup\\actor_payment_table_20200424.sql\r\n\r\nGenerate the backup of database data\r\n\r\nmysqldump -u root -p sakila ‚Äìno-create-info > C:\\MySQLBackup\\sakila_data_only_20200424.sql\r\n\r\nRestore the MySQL Database\r\n\r\nmysql> drop database sakila;\r\nQuery OK, 24 rows affected (0.35 sec)\r\n\r\n\r\nmysql> create database sakila;\r\nQuery OK, 1 row affected (0.01 sec)\r\nmysql>\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen you restore the database, instead of using mysqldump, you must use mysql; otherwise, \r\nthe mysqldump will not generate \r\nthe schema and the data. Execute the following command to restore the sakila database: \r\n\r\n\r\nmysql -u root -p sakila < C:\\MySQLBackup\\sakila_20200424.sql\r\n\r\nmysql> use sakila;\r\nDatabase changed\r\nmysql> show tables;\r\n\r\n\r\nRestore a specific table in the database\r\n\r\n\r\nmysql> use sakila;\r\nDatabase changed\r\nmysql> drop table actor;\r\n\r\n Create a dummy database named sakila_dummy and restore the backup of the sakila database on it. Following is the command.\r\n\r\nmysql> create database sakila_dummy;\r\nmysql> use sakila_dummy;\r\nmysql> source C:\\MySQLBackup\\sakila_20200424.sql\r\n\r\n\r\nStep 2:\r\n\r\nBackup the actor table to sakila_dummy_actor_20200424.sql file. Following is the command\r\n\r\nC:\\Users\\Nisarg> mysqldump -u root -p sakila_dummy actor > C:\\MySQLBackup\\sakila_dummy_actor_20200424.sql\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nStep 3:\r\n\r\nRestore the actor table from the ‚Äúsakila_dummy_actor_20200424.sql‚Äù file. Following is the command on the MySQL command-line tool.\r\n\r\nmysql> source C:\\MySQLBackup\\sakila_dummy_actor_20200424.sql\r\n\r\nExecute the following command to verify the table has been restored successfully.\r\n\r\nmysql> use sakila;\r\nDatabase changed\r\nmysql> show tables;\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n",

    "link": "https://stackoverflow.com/questions/16251501/error-warning-strpos-function-strpos-offset-not-contained-in-string-ca",
    "title": "difference between ajax get and post",
    "description": "\r\n136\r\n\r\nGET is designed for getting data from the server. POST (and lesser-known friends PUT and DELETE) are designed for modifying data on the server.\r\n\r\nA GET request should never cause data to be removed from an application. If you have a link you can click on with a GET to remove data, then Google spidering your site could click on all your \"Delete\" links.\r\n\r\nThe canonical answer can be found here, which quotes the HTML 2.0 spec:\r\n\r\n    If the processing of a form is idempotent (i.e. it has no lasting observable effect on the state of the world), then the form method should be GET. Many database searches have no visible side-effects and make ideal applications of query forms.\r\n\r\n    If the service associated with the processing of a form has side effects (for example, modification of a database or subscription to a service), the method should be POST.\r\n\r\nIn your AJAX call, you need to use whatever method your server supports. You should always design your server so that operations that modify data are called by POST/PUT/DELETE. Other comments have links to REST, which generally maps C/R/U/D to \"POST or PUT\"(Create)/GET(Read)/PUT(Update)/DELETE(Delete).\r\n\r\n\r\nIf you're sending large amounts of data, or sensitive data over HTTPS, you will want to use POST. If it's just a simple parameter, I would use GET.\r\n\r\nGET requests have a limit to the amount of data that can be sent. I forget the exact number, but this can cause issues if you're sending anything substantial.\r\n\r\nBasically the difference between GET and POST is that in a GET request, the parameters are passed in the URL where as in a POST, the parameters are included in the message body.\r\n",
    "reg_date": "2021-10-29T15:22:52.000Z"

Consider this a safety check.\n\nSo this is when PPA Purge comes in picture. It not only disables the PPA but also uninstalls all the programs installed by the PPA\n or revert them to original version provided by your distribution.\n\nInstall ppa-purge by using the following command:\n\nsudo apt-get install ppa-purge\n\nNow use it in following manner to purge the PPA:\n\nsudo ppa-purge ppa-url\nThe URL of the PPA can be found in the Software Sources list\n\nResponses from stack  overflow the same \nUse the --remove flag, similar to how the PPA was added:\n\nsudo add-apt-repository --remove ppa:whatever/ppa\n\nYou can also remove PPAs by deleting the .list files from /etc/apt/sources.list.d directory.\n\nAs a safer alternative, you can install ppa-purge:\n\nsudo apt-get install ppa-purge\n\nAnd then remove the PPA, downgrading gracefully packages it provided to packages provided by official repositories:\n\nsudo ppa-purge ppa:whatever/ppa\n\nNote that this will uninstall packages provided by the PPA, but not those provided by the official repositories.     "title": "git git git git git git git git git git git git git git git git git git git git git git ",
    "description": "Set Up and Initialization\r\n\r\nCheck your Git version with the following command, which will also confirm that Git is installed:\r\n\r\n    git --version\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nGit allows you to configure a number of settings that will apply to all the repositories on your local machine. For instance, configure a username that Git will use to credit you with any changes you make to a local repository:\r\n\r\n    git config --global user.name ‚Äúfirstname lastname‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nConfigure an email address to be associated with each history marker:\r\n\r\n    git config --global user.email ‚Äúvalid-email‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nConfigure your preferred text editor as well:\r\n\r\n    git config --global core.editor ‚Äúnano‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can initialize your current working directory as a Git repository with init:\r\n\r\n    git init\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo copy an existing Git repository hosted remotely, you‚Äôll use git clone with the repo‚Äôs URL or server location (in the latter case you will use ssh):\r\n\r\n    git clone https://www.github.com/username/repo-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow your current Git directory‚Äôs remote repository:\r\n\r\n    git remote\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nFor more verbose output, use the -v flag:\r\n\r\n    git remote -v\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAdd the Git upstream, which can be a URL or can be hosted on a server (in the latter case, connect with ssh):\r\n\r\n    git remote add upstream https://www.github.com/username/repo-name\r\n\r\n \r\nStaging\r\n\r\nWhen you‚Äôve modified a file and have marked it to go in your next commit, it is considered to be a staged file.\r\n\r\nCheck the status of your Git repository, including files added that are not staged, and files that are staged:\r\n\r\n    git status\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo stage modified files, use the add command, which you can run multiple times before a commit. If you make subsequent changes that you want to include in the next commit, you must run add again.\r\n\r\nYou can specify the specific file with add:\r\n\r\n    git add my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nWith . you can add all files in the current directory, including files that begin with a .:\r\n\r\n    git add .\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you would like to add all files in a current directory as well as files in subdirectories, you can use the -all or -A flag:\r\n\r\n    git add -A\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can remove a file from staging while retaining changes within your working directory with reset:\r\n\r\n    git reset my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\nCommitting\r\n\r\nOnce you have staged your updates, you are ready to commit them, which will record changes you have made to the repository.\r\n\r\nTo commit staged files, you‚Äôll run the commit command with your meaningful commit message so that you can track commits:\r\n\r\n    git commit -m \"Commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can condense staging all tracked files by committing them in one step:\r\n\r\n    git commit -am \"Commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you need to modify your commit message, you can do so with the --amend flag:\r\n\r\n    git commit --amend -m \"New commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\nBranches\r\n\r\nA branch in Git is a movable pointer to one of the commits in the repository, it allows you to isolate work and manage feature development and integrations. You can learn more about branches by reading the Git documentation.\r\n\r\nList all current branches with the branch command. An asterisk (*) will appear next to your currently active branch:\r\n\r\n    git branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nCreate a new branch. You will remain on your currently active branch until you switch to the new one:\r\n\r\n    git branch new-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSwitch to any existing branch and check it out into your current working directory:\r\n\r\n    git checkout another-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can consolidate the creation and checkout of a new branch by using the -b flag:\r\n\r\n    git checkout -b new-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nRename your branch name:\r\n\r\n    git branch -m current-branch-name new-branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nMerge the specified branch‚Äôs history into the one you‚Äôre currently working in:\r\n\r\n    git merge branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAbort the merge, in case there are conflicts:\r\n\r\n    git merge --abort\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can also select a particular commit to merge with cherry-pick with the string that references the specific commit:\r\n\r\n    git cherry-pick f7649d0\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nWhen you have merged a branch and no longer need the branch, you can delete it:\r\n\r\n    git branch -d branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you have not merged a branch to main, but are sure you want to delete it, you can force delete a branch:\r\n\r\n    git branch -D branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\nCollaborate and Update\r\n\r\nTo download changes from another repository, such as the remote upstream, you‚Äôll use fetch:\r\n\r\n    git fetch upstream\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nMerge the fetched commits. Note that some repositories may use master instead of main:\r\n\r\n    git merge upstream/main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nPush or transmit your local branch commits to the remote repository branch:\r\n\r\n    git push origin main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nFetch and merge any commits from the tracking remote branch:\r\n\r\n    git pull\r\n\r\n \r\nInspecting\r\n\r\nDisplay the commit history for the currently active branch:\r\n\r\n    git log\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the commits that changed a particular file. This follows the file regardless of file renaming:\r\n\r\n    git log --follow my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the commits that are on one branch and not on the other. This will show commits on a-branch that are not on b-branch:\r\n\r\n    git log a-branch..b-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nLook at reference logs (reflog) to see when the tips of branches and other references were last updated within the repository:\r\n\r\n    git reflog\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow any object in Git via its commit string or hash in a more human-readable format:\r\n\r\n    git show de754f5\r\n\r\n \r\nShow Changes\r\n\r\nThe git diff command shows changes between commits, branches, and more. You can read more fully about it through the Git documentation.\r\n\r\nCompare modified files that are on the staging area:\r\n\r\n    git diff --staged\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nDisplay the diff of what is in a-branch but is not in b-branch:\r\n\r\n    git diff a-branch..b-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the diff between two specific commits:\r\n\r\n    git diff 61ce3e6..e221d9c\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTrack path changes by deleting a file from your project and stage this removal for commit:\r\n\r\n    git rm file\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nOr change an existing file path and then stage the move:\r\n\r\n    git mv existing-path new-path\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nCheck the commit log to see if any paths have been moved:\r\n\r\n    git log --stat -M\r\n\r\n \r\nStashing\r\n\r\nSometimes you‚Äôll find that you made changes to some code, but before you finish you have to begin working on something else. You‚Äôre not quite ready to commit the changes you have made so far, but you don‚Äôt want to lose your work. The git stash command will allow you to save your local modifications and revert back to the working directory that is in line with the most recent HEAD commit.\r\n\r\nStash your current work:\r\n\r\n    git stash\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSee what you currently have stashed:\r\n\r\n    git stash list\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYour stashes will be named stash@{0}, stash@{1}, and so on.\r\n\r\nShow information about a particular stash:\r\n\r\n    git stash show stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo bring the files in a current stash out of the stash while still retaining the stash, use apply:\r\n\r\n    git stash apply stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you want to bring files out of a stash, and no longer need the stash, use pop:\r\n\r\n    git stash pop stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you no longer need the files saved in a particular stash, you can drop the stash:\r\n\r\n    git stash drop stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you have multiple stashes saved and no longer need to use any of them, you can use clear to remove them:\r\n\r\n    git stash clear\r\n\r\n \r\nIgnoring Files\r\n\r\nIf you want to keep files in your local Git directory, but do not want to commit them to the project, you can add these files to your .gitignore file so that they do not cause conflicts.\r\n\r\nUse a text editor such as nano to add files to the .gitignore file:\r\n\r\n    nano .gitignore\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo see examples of .gitignore files, you can look at GitHub‚Äôs .gitignore template repo.\r\nRebasing\r\n\r\nA rebase allows us to move branches around by changing the commit that they are based on. With rebasing, you can squash or reword commits.\r\n\r\nYou can start a rebase by either calling the number of commits you have made that you want to rebase (5 in the case below):\r\n\r\n    git rebase -i HEAD~5\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAlternatively, you can rebase based on a particular commit string or hash:\r\n\r\n    git rebase -i 074a4e5\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nOnce you have squashed or reworded commits, you can complete the rebase of your branch on top of the latest version of the project‚Äôs upstream code. Note that some repositories may use master instead of main:\r\n\r\n    git rebase upstream/main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo learn more about rebasing and updating, you can read How To Rebase and Update a Pull Request, which is also applicable to any type of commit.\r\nReverting and Resetting\r\n\r\nYou can revert back the changes that you made on a given commit by using revert. Your working tree will need to be clean in order for this to be achieved:\r\n\r\n    git revert 1fc6665\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSometimes, including after a rebase, you need to reset your working tree. You can reset to a particular commit, and delete all changes, with the following command:\r\n\r\n    git reset --hard 1fc6665\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo force push your last known non-conflicting commit to the origin repository, you‚Äôll need to use --force:\r\n\r\nWarning: Force pushing to the main (sometimes master) branch is often frowned upon unless there is a really important reason for doing it. Use sparingly when working on your own repositories, and work to avoid this when you‚Äôre collaborating.\r\n\r\n    git push --force origin main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo remove local untracked files and subdirectories from the Git directory for a clean working branch, you can use git clean:\r\n\r\n    git clean -f -d\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you need to modify your local repository so that it looks like the current upstream main branch (that is, there are too many conflicts), you can perform a hard reset:\r\n\r\nNote: Performing this command will make your local repository look exactly like the upstream. Any commits you have made but that were not pulled into the upstream will be destroyed.\r\n\r\n    git reset --hard upstream/main\r\n",

    "description": "Set Up and Initialization\r\n\r\nCheck your Git version with the following command, which will also confirm that Git is installed:\r\n\r\n    git --version\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nGit allows you to configure a number of settings that will apply to all the repositories on your local machine. For instance, configure a username that Git will use to credit you with any changes you make to a local repository:\r\n\r\n    git config --global user.name ‚Äúfirstname lastname‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nConfigure an email address to be associated with each history marker:\r\n\r\n    git config --global user.email ‚Äúvalid-email‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nConfigure your preferred text editor as well:\r\n\r\n    git config --global core.editor ‚Äúnano‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can initialize your current working directory as a Git repository with init:\r\n\r\n    git init\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo copy an existing Git repository hosted remotely, you‚Äôll use git clone with the repo‚Äôs URL or server location (in the latter case you will use ssh):\r\n\r\n    git clone https://www.github.com/username/repo-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow your current Git directory‚Äôs remote repository:\r\n\r\n    git remote\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nFor more verbose output, use the -v flag:\r\n\r\n    git remote -v\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAdd the Git upstream, which can be a URL or can be hosted on a server (in the latter case, connect with ssh):\r\n\r\n    git remote add upstream https://www.github.com/username/repo-name\r\n\r\n \r\nStaging\r\n\r\nWhen you‚Äôve modified a file and have marked it to go in your next commit, it is considered to be a staged file.\r\n\r\nCheck the status of your Git repository, including files added that are not staged, and files that are staged:\r\n\r\n    git status\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo stage modified files, use the add command, which you can run multiple times before a commit. If you make subsequent changes that you want to include in the next commit, you must run add again.\r\n\r\nYou can specify the specific file with add:\r\n\r\n    git add my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nWith . you can add all files in the current directory, including files that begin with a .:\r\n\r\n    git add .\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you would like to add all files in a current directory as well as files in subdirectories, you can use the -all or -A flag:\r\n\r\n    git add -A\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can remove a file from staging while retaining changes within your working directory with reset:\r\n\r\n    git reset my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\nCommitting\r\n\r\nOnce you have staged your updates, you are ready to commit them, which will record changes you have made to the repository.\r\n\r\nTo commit staged files, you‚Äôll run the commit command with your meaningful commit message so that you can track commits:\r\n\r\n    git commit -m \"Commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can condense staging all tracked files by committing them in one step:\r\n\r\n    git commit -am \"Commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you need to modify your commit message, you can do so with the --amend flag:\r\n\r\n    git commit --amend -m \"New commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\nBranches\r\n\r\nA branch in Git is a movable pointer to one of the commits in the repository, it allows you to isolate work and manage feature development and integrations. You can learn more about branches by reading the Git documentation.\r\n\r\nList all current branches with the branch command. An asterisk (*) will appear next to your currently active branch:\r\n\r\n    git branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nCreate a new branch. You will remain on your currently active branch until you switch to the new one:\r\n\r\n    git branch new-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSwitch to any existing branch and check it out into your current working directory:\r\n\r\n    git checkout another-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can consolidate the creation and checkout of a new branch by using the -b flag:\r\n\r\n    git checkout -b new-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nRename your branch name:\r\n\r\n    git branch -m current-branch-name new-branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nMerge the specified branch‚Äôs history into the one you‚Äôre currently working in:\r\n\r\n    git merge branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAbort the merge, in case there are conflicts:\r\n\r\n    git merge --abort\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can also select a particular commit to merge with cherry-pick with the string that references the specific commit:\r\n\r\n    git cherry-pick f7649d0\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nWhen you have merged a branch and no longer need the branch, you can delete it:\r\n\r\n    git branch -d branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you have not merged a branch to main, but are sure you want to delete it, you can force delete a branch:\r\n\r\n    git branch -D branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\nCollaborate and Update\r\n\r\nTo download changes from another repository, such as the remote upstream, you‚Äôll use fetch:\r\n\r\n    git fetch upstream\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nMerge the fetched commits. Note that some repositories may use master instead of main:\r\n\r\n    git merge upstream/main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nPush or transmit your local branch commits to the remote repository branch:\r\n\r\n    git push origin main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nFetch and merge any commits from the tracking remote branch:\r\n\r\n    git pull\r\n\r\n \r\nInspecting\r\n\r\nDisplay the commit history for the currently active branch:\r\n\r\n    git log\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the commits that changed a particular file. This follows the file regardless of file renaming:\r\n\r\n    git log --follow my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the commits that are on one branch and not on the other. This will show commits on a-branch that are not on b-branch:\r\n\r\n    git log a-branch..b-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nLook at reference logs (reflog) to see when the tips of branches and other references were last updated within the repository:\r\n\r\n    git reflog\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow any object in Git via its commit string or hash in a more human-readable format:\r\n\r\n    git show de754f5\r\n\r\n \r\nShow Changes\r\n\r\nThe git diff command shows changes between commits, branches, and more. You can read more fully about it through the Git documentation.\r\n\r\nCompare modified files that are on the staging area:\r\n\r\n    git diff --staged\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nDisplay the diff of what is in a-branch but is not in b-branch:\r\n\r\n    git diff a-branch..b-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the diff between two specific commits:\r\n\r\n    git diff 61ce3e6..e221d9c\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTrack path changes by deleting a file from your project and stage this removal for commit:\r\n\r\n    git rm file\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nOr change an existing file path and then stage the move:\r\n\r\n    git mv existing-path new-path\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nCheck the commit log to see if any paths have been moved:\r\n\r\n    git log --stat -M\r\n\r\n \r\nStashing\r\n\r\nSometimes you‚Äôll find that you made changes to some code, but before you finish you have to begin working on something else. You‚Äôre not quite ready to commit the changes you have made so far, but you don‚Äôt want to lose your work. The git stash command will allow you to save your local modifications and revert back to the working directory that is in line with the most recent HEAD commit.\r\n\r\nStash your current work:\r\n\r\n    git stash\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSee what you currently have stashed:\r\n\r\n    git stash list\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYour stashes will be named stash@{0}, stash@{1}, and so on.\r\n\r\nShow information about a particular stash:\r\n\r\n    git stash show stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo bring the files in a current stash out of the stash while still retaining the stash, use apply:\r\n\r\n    git stash apply stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you want to bring files out of a stash, and no longer need the stash, use pop:\r\n\r\n    git stash pop stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you no longer need the files saved in a particular stash, you can drop the stash:\r\n\r\n    git stash drop stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you have multiple stashes saved and no longer need to use any of them, you can use clear to remove them:\r\n\r\n    git stash clear\r\n\r\n \r\nIgnoring Files\r\n\r\nIf you want to keep files in your local Git directory, but do not want to commit them to the project, you can add these files to your .gitignore file so that they do not cause conflicts.\r\n\r\nUse a text editor such as nano to add files to the .gitignore file:\r\n\r\n    nano .gitignore\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo see examples of .gitignore files, you can look at GitHub‚Äôs .gitignore template repo.\r\nRebasing\r\n\r\nA rebase allows us to move branches around by changing the commit that they are based on. With rebasing, you can squash or reword commits.\r\n\r\nYou can start a rebase by either calling the number of commits you have made that you want to rebase (5 in the case below):\r\n\r\n    git rebase -i HEAD~5\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAlternatively, you can rebase based on a particular commit string or hash:\r\n\r\n    git rebase -i 074a4e5\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nOnce you have squashed or reworded commits, you can complete the rebase of your branch on top of the latest version of the project‚Äôs upstream code. Note that some repositories may use master instead of main:\r\n\r\n    git rebase upstream/main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo learn more about rebasing and updating, you can read How To Rebase and Update a Pull Request, which is also applicable to any type of commit.\r\nReverting and Resetting\r\n\r\nYou can revert back the changes that you made on a given commit by using revert. Your working tree will need to be clean in order for this to be achieved:\r\n\r\n    git revert 1fc6665\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSometimes, including after a rebase, you need to reset your working tree. You can reset to a particular commit, and delete all changes, with the following command:\r\n\r\n    git reset --hard 1fc6665\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo force push your last known non-conflicting commit to the origin repository, you‚Äôll need to use --force:\r\n\r\nWarning: Force pushing to the main (sometimes master) branch is often frowned upon unless there is a really important reason for doing it. Use sparingly when working on your own repositories, and work to avoid this when you‚Äôre collaborating.\r\n\r\n    git push --force origin main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo remove local untracked files and subdirectories from the Git directory for a clean working branch, you can use git clean:\r\n\r\n    git clean -f -d\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you need to modify your local repository so that it looks like the current upstream main branch (that is, there are too many conflicts), you can perform a hard reset:\r\n\r\nNote: Performing this command will make your local repository look exactly like the upstream. Any commits you have made but that were not pulled into the upstream will be destroyed.\r\n\r\n    git reset --hard upstream/main\r\n",

    "description": "Set Up and Initialization\r\n\r\nCheck your Git version with the following command, which will also confirm that Git is installed:\r\n\r\n    git --version\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nGit allows you to configure a number of settings that will apply to all the repositories on your local machine. For instance, configure a username that Git will use to credit you with any changes you make to a local repository:\r\n\r\n    git config --global user.name ‚Äúfirstname lastname‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nConfigure an email address to be associated with each history marker:\r\n\r\n    git config --global user.email ‚Äúvalid-email‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nConfigure your preferred text editor as well:\r\n\r\n    git config --global core.editor ‚Äúnano‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can initialize your current working directory as a Git repository with init:\r\n\r\n    git init\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo copy an existing Git repository hosted remotely, you‚Äôll use git clone with the repo‚Äôs URL or server location (in the latter case you will use ssh):\r\n\r\n    git clone https://www.github.com/username/repo-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow your current Git directory‚Äôs remote repository:\r\n\r\n    git remote\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nFor more verbose output, use the -v flag:\r\n\r\n    git remote -v\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAdd the Git upstream, which can be a URL or can be hosted on a server (in the latter case, connect with ssh):\r\n\r\n    git remote add upstream https://www.github.com/username/repo-name\r\n\r\n \r\nStaging\r\n\r\nWhen you‚Äôve modified a file and have marked it to go in your next commit, it is considered to be a staged file.\r\n\r\nCheck the status of your Git repository, including files added that are not staged, and files that are staged:\r\n\r\n    git status\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo stage modified files, use the add command, which you can run multiple times before a commit. If you make subsequent changes that you want to include in the next commit, you must run add again.\r\n\r\nYou can specify the specific file with add:\r\n\r\n    git add my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nWith . you can add all files in the current directory, including files that begin with a .:\r\n\r\n    git add .\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you would like to add all files in a current directory as well as files in subdirectories, you can use the -all or -A flag:\r\n\r\n    git add -A\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can remove a file from staging while retaining changes within your working directory with reset:\r\n\r\n    git reset my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\nCommitting\r\n\r\nOnce you have staged your updates, you are ready to commit them, which will record changes you have made to the repository.\r\n\r\nTo commit staged files, you‚Äôll run the commit command with your meaningful commit message so that you can track commits:\r\n\r\n    git commit -m \"Commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can condense staging all tracked files by committing them in one step:\r\n\r\n    git commit -am \"Commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you need to modify your commit message, you can do so with the --amend flag:\r\n\r\n    git commit --amend -m \"New commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\nBranches\r\n\r\nA branch in Git is a movable pointer to one of the commits in the repository, it allows you to isolate work and manage feature development and integrations. You can learn more about branches by reading the Git documentation.\r\n\r\nList all current branches with the branch command. An asterisk (*) will appear next to your currently active branch:\r\n\r\n    git branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nCreate a new branch. You will remain on your currently active branch until you switch to the new one:\r\n\r\n    git branch new-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSwitch to any existing branch and check it out into your current working directory:\r\n\r\n    git checkout another-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can consolidate the creation and checkout of a new branch by using the -b flag:\r\n\r\n    git checkout -b new-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nRename your branch name:\r\n\r\n    git branch -m current-branch-name new-branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nMerge the specified branch‚Äôs history into the one you‚Äôre currently working in:\r\n\r\n    git merge branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAbort the merge, in case there are conflicts:\r\n\r\n    git merge --abort\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can also select a particular commit to merge with cherry-pick with the string that references the specific commit:\r\n\r\n    git cherry-pick f7649d0\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nWhen you have merged a branch and no longer need the branch, you can delete it:\r\n\r\n    git branch -d branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you have not merged a branch to main, but are sure you want to delete it, you can force delete a branch:\r\n\r\n    git branch -D branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\nCollaborate and Update\r\n\r\nTo download changes from another repository, such as the remote upstream, you‚Äôll use fetch:\r\n\r\n    git fetch upstream\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nMerge the fetched commits. Note that some repositories may use master instead of main:\r\n\r\n    git merge upstream/main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nPush or transmit your local branch commits to the remote repository branch:\r\n\r\n    git push origin main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nFetch and merge any commits from the tracking remote branch:\r\n\r\n    git pull\r\n\r\n \r\nInspecting\r\n\r\nDisplay the commit history for the currently active branch:\r\n\r\n    git log\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the commits that changed a particular file. This follows the file regardless of file renaming:\r\n\r\n    git log --follow my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the commits that are on one branch and not on the other. This will show commits on a-branch that are not on b-branch:\r\n\r\n    git log a-branch..b-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nLook at reference logs (reflog) to see when the tips of branches and other references were last updated within the repository:\r\n\r\n    git reflog\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow any object in Git via its commit string or hash in a more human-readable format:\r\n\r\n    git show de754f5\r\n\r\n \r\nShow Changes\r\n\r\nThe git diff command shows changes between commits, branches, and more. You can read more fully about it through the Git documentation.\r\n\r\nCompare modified files that are on the staging area:\r\n\r\n    git diff --staged\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nDisplay the diff of what is in a-branch but is not in b-branch:\r\n\r\n    git diff a-branch..b-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the diff between two specific commits:\r\n\r\n    git diff 61ce3e6..e221d9c\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTrack path changes by deleting a file from your project and stage this removal for commit:\r\n\r\n    git rm file\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nOr change an existing file path and then stage the move:\r\n\r\n    git mv existing-path new-path\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nCheck the commit log to see if any paths have been moved:\r\n\r\n    git log --stat -M\r\n\r\n \r\nStashing\r\n\r\nSometimes you‚Äôll find that you made changes to some code, but before you finish you have to begin working on something else. You‚Äôre not quite ready to commit the changes you have made so far, but you don‚Äôt want to lose your work. The git stash command will allow you to save your local modifications and revert back to the working directory that is in line with the most recent HEAD commit.\r\n\r\nStash your current work:\r\n\r\n    git stash\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSee what you currently have stashed:\r\n\r\n    git stash list\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYour stashes will be named stash@{0}, stash@{1}, and so on.\r\n\r\nShow information about a particular stash:\r\n\r\n    git stash show stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo bring the files in a current stash out of the stash while still retaining the stash, use apply:\r\n\r\n    git stash apply stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you want to bring files out of a stash, and no longer need the stash, use pop:\r\n\r\n    git stash pop stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you no longer need the files saved in a particular stash, you can drop the stash:\r\n\r\n    git stash drop stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you have multiple stashes saved and no longer need to use any of them, you can use clear to remove them:\r\n\r\n    git stash clear\r\n\r\n \r\nIgnoring Files\r\n\r\nIf you want to keep files in your local Git directory, but do not want to commit them to the project, you can add these files to your .gitignore file so that they do not cause conflicts.\r\n\r\nUse a text editor such as nano to add files to the .gitignore file:\r\n\r\n    nano .gitignore\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo see examples of .gitignore files, you can look at GitHub‚Äôs .gitignore template repo.\r\nRebasing\r\n\r\nA rebase allows us to move branches around by changing the commit that they are based on. With rebasing, you can squash or reword commits.\r\n\r\nYou can start a rebase by either calling the number of commits you have made that you want to rebase (5 in the case below):\r\n\r\n    git rebase -i HEAD~5\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAlternatively, you can rebase based on a particular commit string or hash:\r\n\r\n    git rebase -i 074a4e5\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nOnce you have squashed or reworded commits, you can complete the rebase of your branch on top of the latest version of the project‚Äôs upstream code. Note that some repositories may use master instead of main:\r\n\r\n    git rebase upstream/main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo learn more about rebasing and updating, you can read How To Rebase and Update a Pull Request, which is also applicable to any type of commit.\r\nReverting and Resetting\r\n\r\nYou can revert back the changes that you made on a given commit by using revert. Your working tree will need to be clean in order for this to be achieved:\r\n\r\n    git revert 1fc6665\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSometimes, including after a rebase, you need to reset your working tree. You can reset to a particular commit, and delete all changes, with the following command:\r\n\r\n    git reset --hard 1fc6665\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo force push your last known non-conflicting commit to the origin repository, you‚Äôll need to use --force:\r\n\r\nWarning: Force pushing to the main (sometimes master) branch is often frowned upon unless there is a really important reason for doing it. Use sparingly when working on your own repositories, and work to avoid this when you‚Äôre collaborating.\r\n\r\n    git push --force origin main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo remove local untracked files and subdirectories from the Git directory for a clean working branch, you can use git clean:\r\n\r\n    git clean -f -d\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you need to modify your local repository so that it looks like the current upstream main branch (that is, there are too many conflicts), you can perform a hard reset:\r\n\r\nNote: Performing this command will make your local repository look exactly like the upstream. Any commits you have made but that were not pulled into the upstream will be destroyed.\r\n\r\n    git reset --hard upstream/main\r\n",

    "description": "Set Up and Initialization\r\n\r\nCheck your Git version with the following command, which will also confirm that Git is installed:\r\n\r\n    git --version\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nGit allows you to configure a number of settings that will apply to all the repositories on your local machine. For instance, configure a username that Git will use to credit you with any changes you make to a local repository:\r\n\r\n    git config --global user.name ‚Äúfirstname lastname‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nConfigure an email address to be associated with each history marker:\r\n\r\n    git config --global user.email ‚Äúvalid-email‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nConfigure your preferred text editor as well:\r\n\r\n    git config --global core.editor ‚Äúnano‚Äù\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can initialize your current working directory as a Git repository with init:\r\n\r\n    git init\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo copy an existing Git repository hosted remotely, you‚Äôll use git clone with the repo‚Äôs URL or server location (in the latter case you will use ssh):\r\n\r\n    git clone https://www.github.com/username/repo-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow your current Git directory‚Äôs remote repository:\r\n\r\n    git remote\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nFor more verbose output, use the -v flag:\r\n\r\n    git remote -v\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAdd the Git upstream, which can be a URL or can be hosted on a server (in the latter case, connect with ssh):\r\n\r\n    git remote add upstream https://www.github.com/username/repo-name\r\n\r\n \r\nStaging\r\n\r\nWhen you‚Äôve modified a file and have marked it to go in your next commit, it is considered to be a staged file.\r\n\r\nCheck the status of your Git repository, including files added that are not staged, and files that are staged:\r\n\r\n    git status\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo stage modified files, use the add command, which you can run multiple times before a commit. If you make subsequent changes that you want to include in the next commit, you must run add again.\r\n\r\nYou can specify the specific file with add:\r\n\r\n    git add my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nWith . you can add all files in the current directory, including files that begin with a .:\r\n\r\n    git add .\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you would like to add all files in a current directory as well as files in subdirectories, you can use the -all or -A flag:\r\n\r\n    git add -A\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can remove a file from staging while retaining changes within your working directory with reset:\r\n\r\n    git reset my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\nCommitting\r\n\r\nOnce you have staged your updates, you are ready to commit them, which will record changes you have made to the repository.\r\n\r\nTo commit staged files, you‚Äôll run the commit command with your meaningful commit message so that you can track commits:\r\n\r\n    git commit -m \"Commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can condense staging all tracked files by committing them in one step:\r\n\r\n    git commit -am \"Commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you need to modify your commit message, you can do so with the --amend flag:\r\n\r\n    git commit --amend -m \"New commit message\"\r\n\r\n \r\nBranches\r\n\r\nA branch in Git is a movable pointer to one of the commits in the repository, it allows you to isolate work and manage feature development and integrations. You can learn more about branches by reading the Git documentation.\r\n\r\nList all current branches with the branch command. An asterisk (*) will appear next to your currently active branch:\r\n\r\n    git branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nCreate a new branch. You will remain on your currently active branch until you switch to the new one:\r\n\r\n    git branch new-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSwitch to any existing branch and check it out into your current working directory:\r\n\r\n    git checkout another-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can consolidate the creation and checkout of a new branch by using the -b flag:\r\n\r\n    git checkout -b new-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nRename your branch name:\r\n\r\n    git branch -m current-branch-name new-branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nMerge the specified branch‚Äôs history into the one you‚Äôre currently working in:\r\n\r\n    git merge branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAbort the merge, in case there are conflicts:\r\n\r\n    git merge --abort\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYou can also select a particular commit to merge with cherry-pick with the string that references the specific commit:\r\n\r\n    git cherry-pick f7649d0\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nWhen you have merged a branch and no longer need the branch, you can delete it:\r\n\r\n    git branch -d branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you have not merged a branch to main, but are sure you want to delete it, you can force delete a branch:\r\n\r\n    git branch -D branch-name\r\n\r\n \r\nCollaborate and Update\r\n\r\nTo download changes from another repository, such as the remote upstream, you‚Äôll use fetch:\r\n\r\n    git fetch upstream\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nMerge the fetched commits. Note that some repositories may use master instead of main:\r\n\r\n    git merge upstream/main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nPush or transmit your local branch commits to the remote repository branch:\r\n\r\n    git push origin main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nFetch and merge any commits from the tracking remote branch:\r\n\r\n    git pull\r\n\r\n \r\nInspecting\r\n\r\nDisplay the commit history for the currently active branch:\r\n\r\n    git log\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the commits that changed a particular file. This follows the file regardless of file renaming:\r\n\r\n    git log --follow my_script.py\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the commits that are on one branch and not on the other. This will show commits on a-branch that are not on b-branch:\r\n\r\n    git log a-branch..b-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nLook at reference logs (reflog) to see when the tips of branches and other references were last updated within the repository:\r\n\r\n    git reflog\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow any object in Git via its commit string or hash in a more human-readable format:\r\n\r\n    git show de754f5\r\n\r\n \r\nShow Changes\r\n\r\nThe git diff command shows changes between commits, branches, and more. You can read more fully about it through the Git documentation.\r\n\r\nCompare modified files that are on the staging area:\r\n\r\n    git diff --staged\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nDisplay the diff of what is in a-branch but is not in b-branch:\r\n\r\n    git diff a-branch..b-branch\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nShow the diff between two specific commits:\r\n\r\n    git diff 61ce3e6..e221d9c\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTrack path changes by deleting a file from your project and stage this removal for commit:\r\n\r\n    git rm file\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nOr change an existing file path and then stage the move:\r\n\r\n    git mv existing-path new-path\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nCheck the commit log to see if any paths have been moved:\r\n\r\n    git log --stat -M\r\n\r\n \r\nStashing\r\n\r\nSometimes you‚Äôll find that you made changes to some code, but before you finish you have to begin working on something else. You‚Äôre not quite ready to commit the changes you have made so far, but you don‚Äôt want to lose your work. The git stash command will allow you to save your local modifications and revert back to the working directory that is in line with the most recent HEAD commit.\r\n\r\nStash your current work:\r\n\r\n    git stash\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSee what you currently have stashed:\r\n\r\n    git stash list\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nYour stashes will be named stash@{0}, stash@{1}, and so on.\r\n\r\nShow information about a particular stash:\r\n\r\n    git stash show stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo bring the files in a current stash out of the stash while still retaining the stash, use apply:\r\n\r\n    git stash apply stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you want to bring files out of a stash, and no longer need the stash, use pop:\r\n\r\n    git stash pop stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you no longer need the files saved in a particular stash, you can drop the stash:\r\n\r\n    git stash drop stash@{0}\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you have multiple stashes saved and no longer need to use any of them, you can use clear to remove them:\r\n\r\n    git stash clear\r\n\r\n \r\nIgnoring Files\r\n\r\nIf you want to keep files in your local Git directory, but do not want to commit them to the project, you can add these files to your .gitignore file so that they do not cause conflicts.\r\n\r\nUse a text editor such as nano to add files to the .gitignore file:\r\n\r\n    nano .gitignore\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo see examples of .gitignore files, you can look at GitHub‚Äôs .gitignore template repo.\r\nRebasing\r\n\r\nA rebase allows us to move branches around by changing the commit that they are based on. With rebasing, you can squash or reword commits.\r\n\r\nYou can start a rebase by either calling the number of commits you have made that you want to rebase (5 in the case below):\r\n\r\n    git rebase -i HEAD~5\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nAlternatively, you can rebase based on a particular commit string or hash:\r\n\r\n    git rebase -i 074a4e5\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nOnce you have squashed or reworded commits, you can complete the rebase of your branch on top of the latest version of the project‚Äôs upstream code. Note that some repositories may use master instead of main:\r\n\r\n    git rebase upstream/main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo learn more about rebasing and updating, you can read How To Rebase and Update a Pull Request, which is also applicable to any type of commit.\r\nReverting and Resetting\r\n\r\nYou can revert back the changes that you made on a given commit by using revert. Your working tree will need to be clean in order for this to be achieved:\r\n\r\n    git revert 1fc6665\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nSometimes, including after a rebase, you need to reset your working tree. You can reset to a particular commit, and delete all changes, with the following command:\r\n\r\n    git reset --hard 1fc6665\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo force push your last known non-conflicting commit to the origin repository, you‚Äôll need to use --force:\r\n\r\nWarning: Force pushing to the main (sometimes master) branch is often frowned upon unless there is a really important reason for doing it. Use sparingly when working on your own repositories, and work to avoid this when you‚Äôre collaborating.\r\n\r\n    git push --force origin main\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nTo remove local untracked files and subdirectories from the Git directory for a clean working branch, you can use git clean:\r\n\r\n    git clean -f -d\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\nIf you need to modify your local repository so that it looks like the current upstream main branch (that is, there are too many conflicts), you can perform a hard reset:\r\n\r\nNote: Performing this command will make your local repository look exactly like the upstream. Any commits you have made but that were not pulled into the upstream will be destroyed.\r\n\r\n    git reset --hard upstream/main\r\n",

    "title": "How To Find Files Modified In The Last N Days Or Minutes Using find",
    "description": "To find the files that have been changed (with the files data modification time older than) in the last N days from a directory and subdirectories, use:\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mtime -N -ls\r\n\r\n    Find all files modified in the last day (24 hours; between now and a day ago) in a directory and subdirectories:\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mtime -1 -ls\r\n\r\n-mtime -1 is the same as -mtime 0.\r\n\r\n    Find all files modified in the last 30 days:\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mtime -30 -ls\r\n\r\nBut what if you need to find the files that have a modification date older than N, for example older than 30 days? In that case you need to use +N instead of -N, like this:\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mtime +N -ls\r\n\r\nExamples:\r\n\r\n    Find all files with a modification date older than 7 days:\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mtime +7 -ls\r\n\r\n    Find all files modified more than 48 hours ago (at least 2 days ago):\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mtime +1 -ls\r\n\r\n    Find all files modified between 24 and 48 hours ago (between 1 and 2 days ago):\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mtime 1 -ls\r\n\r\nSo why is 1 one day ago, and +1 older than 2 days / 48 hours ago? That's because according to the man find, any fractional parts are ignored, so if a file was last modified 1 day and 23 hours ago, -mtime +1 won't match it, treating it as if the file was last modified 1 day, 0 hours, 0 minutes, and 0 seconds ago; see this explanation on why that's the case\r\n\r\nhis being the case, how can you get all files modified at least 1 day ago? Use +0:\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mtime +0 -ls\r\n\r\n\r\nUsing minutes instead of days\r\n\r\nTo find the files that have been modified N minutes ago, or with a modification date older than N, simply replace -mtime with -mmin.\r\n\r\nSo if you want to find the files that have been changed (with the files data modification time older than) in the last N minutes from a directory and subdirectories, use:\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mmin N -ls\r\n\r\nExamples:\r\n\r\n    Find all files modified in the last 5 minutes in a directory and subdirectories:\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mmin -5 -ls\r\n\r\n    Find all files with a modification date older than 5 minutes:\r\n\r\nfind /directory/path/ -mmin +5 -ls\r\n",

    "description": "An async function returns a promise, like in this example:\r\n\r\nconst doSomethingAsync = () => {\r\n  return new Promise(resolve => {\r\n    setTimeout(() => resolve('I did something'), 3000)\r\n  })\r\n}           \r\nWhen you want to call this function you prepend await, and the calling code will stop until the promise is resolved or rejected. One caveat: the client function must be defined as async. Here's an example:\r\n\r\nconst doSomething = async () => {\r\n  console.log(await doSomethingAsync())\r\n}\r\n\r\nThis is a simple example of async/await used to run a function asynchronously:\r\nconst doSomethingAsync = () => {\r\n  return new Promise(resolve => {\r\n    setTimeout(() => resolve('I did something'), 3000)\r\n  })\r\n}\r\n\r\nconst doSomething = async () => {\r\n  console.log(await doSomethingAsync())\r\n}\r\n\r\nconsole.log('Before')\r\ndoSomething()\r\nconsole.log('After')\r\n\r\nPrepending the async keyword to any function means that the function will return a promise.\r\n\r\nEven if it's not doing so explicitly, it will internally make it return a promise.\r\n\r\nThis is why this code is valid:\r\n\r\nJS\r\n\r\nconst aFunction = async () => {\r\n  return 'test'\r\n}\r\n\r\naFunction().then(alert) // This will alert 'test'\r\nand it's the same as:\r\n\r\nJS\r\n\r\nconst aFunction = () => {\r\n  return Promise.resolve('test')\r\n}\r\n\r\naFunction().then(alert) // This will alert 'test'\r\n\r\nAnd this is a very simple example, the major benefits will arise when the code is much more complex.\r\n\r\nFor example here's how you would get a JSON resource, and parse it, using promises:\r\n\r\nJS\r\n\r\nconst getFirstUserData = () => {\r\n  return fetch('/users.json') // get users list\r\n    .then(response => response.json()) // parse JSON\r\n    .then(users => users[0]) // pick first user\r\n    .then(user => fetch(`/users/${user.name}`)) // get user data\r\n    .then(userResponse => userResponse.json()) // parse JSON\r\n}\r\n\r\ngetFirstUserData()\r\n\r\nMultiple async functions in series\r\n\r\nAsync functions can be chained very easily, and the syntax is much more readable than with plain promises:\r\n\r\nconst promiseToDoSomething = () => {\r\n  return new Promise(resolve => {\r\n    setTimeout(() => resolve('I did something'), 10000)\r\n  })\r\n}\r\n\r\nconst watchOverSomeoneDoingSomething = async () => {\r\n  const something = await promiseToDoSomething()\r\n  return something + '\\nand I watched'\r\n}\r\n\r\nconst watchOverSomeoneWatchingSomeoneDoingSomething = async () => {\r\n  const something = await watchOverSomeoneDoingSomething()\r\n  return something + '\\nand I watched as well'\r\n}\r\n\r\nwatchOverSomeoneWatchingSomeoneDoingSomething().then(res => {\r\n  console.log(res)\r\n})\r\n\r\nEasier debugging\r\n\r\nDebugging promises is hard because the debugger will not step over asynchronous code.\r\n\r\nAsync/await makes this very easy because to the compiler it's just like synchronous code.",

It is the libraries/frameworks that create Promises for the clients to consume.\r\n\r\nStill, it is good to understand what goes behind creating a Promise:\r\n`\r\nlet promise = new Promise(function(resolve, reject) {\r\n    // Some imaginary 2000 ms timeout simulating a db call\r\n    setTimeout(()=> {\r\n        if (/* if promise can be fulfilled */) {\r\n            resolve({msg: 'It works', data: 'some data'});\r\n        } else {\r\n            // If promise can not be fulfilled due to some errors like network failure\r\n            reject(new Error({msg: 'It does not work'}));\r\n        }\r\n    }, 2000);\r\n});\r\nvar str_explain_001 =`The promise constructor receives an argument - a callback.\r\n The callback can be a regular function or an arrow function. \r\n The callback takes two parameters - resolve and reject. \r\n Both are function references. The callback is also called the executor.\r\n\r\nThe executor runs immediately when a promise is created.\r\n The promise is resolved by calling resolve() \r\n if the promise is fulfilled, \r\n \tand rejected by calling reject() \r\n if it can't be fulfilled.\r\n\r\nBoth resolve() and reject() \r\ntakes one argument - boolean, string, number, array, or an object.\r\n\r\nConsuming a Promise\r\n\r\nThrough an API, say we requested some data from the server \r\nand it's uncertain when it'll be returned - \r\nif it'll be returned at all. \r\nThis is a perfect example of when we'd     "description": "\r\n Let‚Äôs look at a first example, to give you a taste of what working with Promises is like.\r\n\r\nWith Node.js-style callbacks, reading a file asynchronously looks like this:\r\n\r\nfs.readFile('config.json',\r\n    function (error, text) {\r\n        if (error) {\r\n            console.error('Error while reading config file');\r\n        } else {\r\n            try {\r\n                const obj = JSON.parse(text);\r\n                console.log(JSON.stringify(obj, null, 4));\r\n            } catch (e) {\r\n                console.error('Invalid JSON in file');\r\n            }\r\n        }\r\n    });\r\n\r\nWith Promises, the same functionality is used like this:\r\n\r\nreadFilePromisified('config.json')\r\n.then(function (text) { // (A)\r\n    const obj = JSON.parse(text);\r\n    console.log(JSON.stringify(obj, null, 4));\r\n})\r\n.catch(function (error) { // (B)\r\n    // File read error or JSON SyntaxError\r\n    console.error('An error occurred', error);\r\n});\r\n\r\nExamples\r\nimport {readFile} from 'fs';\r\n\r\nfunction readFilePromisified(filename) {\r\n    return new Promise(\r\n        function (resolve, reject) {\r\n            readFile(filename, { encoding: 'utf8' },\r\n                (error, data) => {\r\n                    if (error) {\r\n                        reject(error);\r\n                    } else {\r\n                        resolve(data);\r\n                    }\r\n                });\r\n        });\r\n}\r\nfunction httpGet(url) {\r\n    return new Promise(\r\n        function (resolve, reject) {\r\n            const request = new XMLHttpRequest();\r\n            request.onload = function () {\r\n                if (this.status === 200) {\r\n                    // Success\r\n                    resolve(this.response);\r\n                } else {\r\n                    // Something went wrong (404 etc.)\r\n                    reject(new Error(this.statusText));\r\n                }\r\n            };\r\n            request.onerror = function () {\r\n                reject(new Error(\r\n                    'XMLHttpRequest Error: '+this.statusText));\r\n            };\r\n            request.open('GET', url);\r\n            request.send();\r\n        });\r\n}\r\n\r\nThis is how you use httpGet():\r\n\r\nhttpGet('http://example.com/file.txt')\r\n.then(\r\n    function (value) {\r\n        console.log('Contents: ' + value);\r\n    },\r\n    function (reason) {\r\n        console.error('Something went wrong', reason);\r\n    });\r\n\r\nLet‚Äôs implement setTimeout() as the Promise-based function delay() (similar to Q.delay()).\r\n\r\nfunction delay(ms) {\r\n    return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {\r\n        setTimeout(resolve, ms); // (A)\r\n    });\r\n}\r\n\r\n// Using delay():\r\ndelay(5000).then(function () { // (B)\r\n    console.log('5 seconds have passed!')\r\n});\r\n\r\n\r\nExample: timing out a Promise\r\n\r\nfunction timeout(ms, promise) {\r\n    return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {\r\n        promise.then(resolve);\r\n        setTimeout(function () {\r\n            reject(new Error('Timeout after '+ms+' ms')); // (A)\r\n        }, ms);\r\n    });\r\n}\r\n\r\n\r\nUsing timeout() looks like this:\r\n\r\ntimeout(5000, httpGet('http://example.com/file.txt'))\r\n.then(function (value) {\r\n    console.log('Contents: ' + value);\r\n})\r\n.catch(function (reason) {\r\n    console.error('Error or timeout', reason);\r\n});\r\n\r\n\r\nclass Model {\r\n    insertInto(db) {\r\n        return db.insert(this.fields) // (A)\r\n        .then(resultCode => {\r\n            this.notifyObservers({event: 'created', model: this});\r\n            return resultCode; // (B)\r\n        });\r\n    }\r\n    ¬∑¬∑¬∑\r\n}\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nLet‚Äôs look at three ways of understanding Promises.\r\n\r\nThe following code contains a Promise-based function asyncFunc() and its invocation.\r\n\r\nfunction asyncFunc() {\r\n    return new Promise((resolve, reject) => { // (A)\r\n        setTimeout(() => resolve('DONE'), 100); // (B)\r\n    });\r\n}\r\nasyncFunc()\r\n.then(x => console.log('Result: '+x));\r\n\r\n// Output:\r\n// Result: DONE\r\n\r\nasyncFunc() returns a Promise.\r\n Once the actual result 'DONE' of the asynchronous computation is ready,\r\n it is delivered via resolve() (line B), which is a parameter of the callback that starts in line A.\r\n\r\nSo what is a Promise?\r\n\r\n    Conceptually, invoking asyncFunc() is a blocking function call.\r\n    A Promise is both a container for a value and an event emitter.\r\n\r\nThe following code invokes asyncFunc() from the async function main(). \r\nasync function main() {\r\n    const x = await asyncFunc(); // (A)\r\n    console.log('Result: '+x); // (B)\r\n\r\n    // Same as:\r\n    // asyncFunc()\r\n    // .then(x => console.log('Result: '+x));\r\n}\r\nmain();\r\n\r\nThe body of main() expresses well what‚Äôs going on conceptually,\r\n how we usually think about asynchronous computations.\r\n Namely, asyncFunc() is a blocking function call:\r\n\r\n    Line A: Wait until asyncFunc() is finished.\r\n    Line B: Then log its result x.\r\n\r\n 25.4.2 A Promise is a container for an asynchronously delivered value\r\n\r\nIf a function returns a Promise then that Promise is like a blank into which the function will (usually) fill in its result, once it has computed it. You can simulate a simple version of this process via an Array:\r\n\r\nfunction asyncFunc() {\r\n    const blank = [];\r\n    setTimeout(() => blank.push('DONE'), 100);\r\n    return blank;\r\n}\r\nconst blank = asyncFunc();\r\n// Wait until the value has been filled in\r\nsetTimeout(() => {\r\n    const x = blank[0]; // (A)\r\n    console.log('Result: '+x);\r\n}, 200);\r\n\r\nWith Promises, you don‚Äôt access the eventual value via [0] (as in line A), you use method then() and a callback.\r\n25.4.3 A Promise is an event emitter\r\n\r\nAnother way to view a Promise is as an object that emits events.\r\n\r\nfunction asyncFunc() {\r\n    const eventEmitter = { success: [] };\r\n    setTimeout(() => { // (A)\r\n        for (const handler of eventEmitter.success) {\r\n            handler('DONE');\r\n        }\r\n    }, 100);\r\n    return eventEmitter;\r\n}\r\nasyncFunc()\r\n.success.push(x => console.log('Result: '+x)); // (B)\r\n\r\nRegistering the event listener (line B) can be done after calling asyncFunc(), \r\nbecause the callback handed to setTimeout() (line A) is executed asynchronously (after this piece of code is finished).\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n",

    "title": "using promise  to properly return a result from mysql with Node?",
    "description": "\r\n\r\n\r\nI guess what you really want to do here is returning a Promise object with the results. This way you can deal with the async operation of retrieving data from the DBMS: when you have the results, you make use of the Promise resolve function to somehow \"return the value\" / \"resolve the promise\".\r\n\r\nHere's an example:\r\n\r\ngetEmployeeNames = function(){\r\n  return new Promise(function(resolve, reject){\r\n    connection.query(\r\n        \"SELECT Name, Surname FROM Employee\", \r\n        function(err, rows){                                                \r\n            if(rows === undefined){\r\n                reject(new Error(\"Error rows is undefined\"));\r\n            }else{\r\n                resolve(rows);\r\n            }\r\n        }\r\n    )}\r\n)}\r\n\r\nOn the caller side, you use the then function to manage fulfillment, and the catch function to manage rejection.\r\n\r\nHere's an example that makes use of the code above:\r\n\r\ngetEmployeeNames()\r\n.then(function(results){\r\n  render(results)\r\n})\r\n.catch(function(err){\r\n  console.log(\"Promise rejection error: \"+err);\r\n})\r\n\r\nAt this point you can set up the view for your results (which are indeed returned as an array of objects):\r\n\r\nrender = function(results){ for (var i in results) console.log(results[i].Name) }\r\n\r\nEdit I'm adding a basic example on how to return HTML content with the results, which is a more typical scenario for Node. Just use the then function of the promise to set the HTTP response, and open your browser at http://localhost:3001\r\n\r\nrequire('http').createServer( function(req, res){\r\nif(req.method == 'GET'){\r\n    if(req.url == '/'){\r\n        res.setHeader('Content-type', 'text/html');\r\n        getEmployeeNames()\r\n        .then(function(results){\r\n          html = \"<h2>\"+results.length+\" employees found</h2>\"\r\n          html += \"<ul>\"\r\n          for (var i in results) html += \"<li>\" + results[i].Name + \" \" +results[i].Surname + \"</li>\";\r\n          html += \"</ul>\"\r\n          res.end(html);\r\n        })\r\n        .catch(function(err){\r\n          console.log(\"Promise rejection error: \"+err);\r\n          res.end(\"<h1>ERROR</h1>\")\r\n        })\r\n    }\r\n}\r\n}).listen(3001)\r\n\r\n\r\n                    ",

    "description": "to keep your code DRY you can keep all your repeating functions into helper modules.\r\n\r\nThe structure can be something like this:\r\n\r\ncontrollers  \r\n‚îú‚îÄ‚îÄ helpers  \r\n    ‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ index.js  \r\n‚îú‚îÄ‚îÄ controller1.js   \r\n‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ controller2.js  \r\n\r\nIn the \"index.js\" helper module you can include your function like this:\r\n\r\nexports.yourFunction = function(args){\r\n...\r\n};\r\n\r\nAnd you can call it in the controllers like this:\r\n\r\nvar helpers = require(\"./helpers\");\r\n...\r\nhelpers.yourFunction();\r\n\r\nAlso, you can find other related answers in this thread:\r\n\r\nJavascript - Best way to structure helpers functions in NodeJS    \r\n\r\nTo provide an example of what a text.js file made up of text-based utility functions might look like:\r\n\r\nmodule.exports = {\r\n    cleanText:function(text) {\r\n        // clean it and return\r\n    },\r\n\r\n    isWithinRange(text, min, max) {\r\n        // check if text is between min and max length\r\n    }\r\n}\r\n\r\nAlternatively, you could do it this way:\r\n\r\nexports.cleanText = function(text) {\r\n    // clean it and return\r\n}\r\n\r\nexports.isWithinRange = function (text, min, max) {\r\n    // check if text is between min and max length\r\n}\r\n\r\nStructuring utility category files to make a larger utility library\r\n\r\nAs far as organizing the utility methods, Luca's example is nice. This library have file sustem structure like your.\r\n\r\nIt divides the functions in categories. Each category is a folder with an index.js file that includes into a namespace (literal object) each functions for that category!\r\n\r\nLodash/\r\n   Objects/\r\n       Function1.js\r\n       Functions2.js\r\n       ....\r\n       Index.js\r\n   Array/\r\n       Function1.js\r\n       ...\r\n       Index.js\r\n\r\nThen in your code you can do this:\r\n\r\nvar objectsUtils = require(\"lodash/objects\");\r\nvar foreach = require(\"lodash/array/each\");\r\n\r\nYou can create a similar file system structure in order to have more flexibility.
